That condition is reminiscent of numbers that are "3-perfect" in the sense of sum of three largest divisors equals the number. Let's analyze the condition for N to be a fixed point.

Let N have at least three proper divisors. Write the three smallest divisors >1 as p, q, r (in increasing order). Then f(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r. For N to be a fixed point, we need N = N/p + N/q + N/r, i.e., 1 = 1/p + 1/q + 1/r. So the condition is that the reciprocals of the three smallest divisors >1 sum to 1. Since p, q, r are integers >=2, and p < q < r. Also, note that if N has exactly 3 proper divisors, then the divisors are 1, p, N, and the three largest proper divisors are p, 1, and N? For p^3, divisors: 1, p, p^2, p^3. Proper: 1, p, p^2. So d2=p, d3=p^2, and N/d2 = p^2, N/d3 = p, N/d4 = 1. So f(N)=p^2+p+1. For N to be fixed, we need p^3 = p^2+p+1, i.e., p^3 - p^2 - p -1 =0. For p=2, 8-4-2-1=1, not 0; p=3, 27-9-3-1=14; p=5, 125-25-5-1=94; so no solution. For N=p*q, divisors: 1, p, q, pq. Proper: 1, p, q. So d2=p, d3=q, and N/d2 = q, N/d3 = p, N/d4 = 1. So f(N)=p+q+1. For N to be fixed, we need pq = p+q+1, i.e., pq - p - q =1, (p-1)(q-1)=2. So p-1=1, q-1=2 => p=2, q=3 gives N=6. So N=6 is the only fixed point with exactly 3 proper divisors. So for N=6, p=2, q=3, r? Actually, we have only two divisors >1, so the condition 1=1/p+1/q+1/r would require r infinite? But we can treat it as special case. So 6 is a fixed point.

Now, for N with at least 4 proper divisors, we have three smallest divisors >1: p, q, r. Then f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r. For N to be fixed, we need N = N/p+N/q+N/r, i.e., 1 = 1/p+1/q+1/r. So the condition is that the reciprocals of the three smallest divisors >1 sum to 1. Since p, q, r are integers >=2, and p<q<r. The only possibilities for 1/p+1/q+1/r =1 are limited. Let's solve.

We need 1/p+1/q+1/r =1. Since p>=2, q>=3, r>=4. The sum of reciprocals of 2,3,4 is 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 6/12+4/12+3/12=13/12 >1. So we need larger numbers. The sum decreases as numbers increase. So we need to find all triples of integers (p,q,r) with 2<=p<q<r such that 1/p+1/q+1/r =1. This is a classic Egyptian fraction representation. Let's find all solutions.

We have 1/p+1/q+1/r =1. Since p is the smallest, p>=2. If p=2, then 1/2+1/q+1/r =1 => 1/q+1/r =1/2. Now, q>=3. If q=3, then 1/3+1/r =1/2 => 1/r =1/2-1/3=1/6 => r=6. So (2,3,6) is a solution. If q=4, then 1/4+1/r =1/2 => 1/r =1/4 => r=4, but q<r required, so no. If q=5, then 1/5+1/r =1/2 => 1/r =3/10 => r=10/3 not integer. q=6: 1/6+1/r=1/2 => 1/r=1/3 => r=3, but r>q? No. So only q=3 gives r=6. So (2,3,6) works.

If p=3, then 1/3+1/q+1/r =1 => 1/q+1/r =2/3. Since q>=4. If q=4, then 1/4+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/4=8/12-3/12=5/12 => r=12/5 not integer. q=5: 1/5+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/5=10/15-3/15=7/15 => r=15/7 not integer. q=6: 1/6+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/6=4/6-1/6=3/6=1/2 => r=2, but r>q? No. q=7: 1/7+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/7=14/21-3/21=11/21 => r=21/11 not integer. q=8: 1/8+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/8=16/24-3/24=13/24 => r=24/13 not integer. q=9: 1/9+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/9=6/9-1/9=5/9 => r=9/5 not integer. q=10: 1/10+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/10=20/30-3/30=17/30 => r=30/17 not integer. q=11: 1/11+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/11=22/33-3/33=19/33 => r=33/19 not integer. q=12: 1/12+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/12=8/12-1/12=7/12 => r=12/7 not integer. q=13: 1/13+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/13=26/39-3/39=23/39 => r=39/23 not integer. q=14: 1/14+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/14=28/42-3/42=25/42 => r=42/25 not integer. q=15: 1/15+1/r=2/3 => 1/r=2/3-1/15=10/15-1/15=9/15=3/5 => r=5/3 not integer. So no solutions for p=3.

If p=4, then 1/4+1/q+1/r =1 => 1/q+1/r =3/4. Since q>=5. q=5: 1/5+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/5=15/20-4/20=11/20 => r=20/11 not integer. q=6: 1/6+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/6=9/12-2/12=7/12 => r=12/7 not integer. q=7: 1/7+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/7=21/28-4/28=17/28 => r=28/17 not integer. q=8: 1/8+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/8=6/8-1/8=5/8 => r=8/5 not integer. q=9: 1/9+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/9=27/36-4/36=23/36 => r=36/23 not integer. q=10: 1/10+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/10=15/20-2/20=13/20 => r=20/13 not integer. q=11: 1/11+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/11=33/44-4/44=29/44 => r=44/29 not integer. q=12: 1/12+1/r=3/4 => 1/r=3/4-1/12=9/12-1/12=8/12=2/3 => r=3/2 not integer. Now, we also need to consider numbers that are not fixed points but eventually reach a fixed point. For example, a1=72 leads to a2=78, which is a fixed point. So a1=72 works. This is a kind of "good" set.

Let's analyze the transformation more generally. For N with at least 3 proper divisors, let the three smallest divisors >1 be p, q, r (with p<q<r). Then f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r. Note that p is the smallest prime factor of N. Also, q is the next smallest divisor >1. It could be p^2 if p^2|N, or the next prime factor. r is the next smallest divisor after q. So f(N) depends on the divisor structure.

We want to find all N such that iterating f eventually stays in numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. Since the fixed points are all numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5, we need to see if any number that is not of that form can eventually reach such a fixed point. So 72 is a special preimage. Are there other preimages of fixed points? For example, consider N such that f(N) = 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5. We need to find all N for which f(N) is a fixed point. Then we can iterate: if a1 leads to a fixed point after some steps, then it's good. So the set of good a1 is the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. Since the fixed points are all numbers of that form, and the recurrence is deterministic, we need to find all numbers in the preimage tree of these fixed points. However, the problem asks: "Determine all possible values of a1." That is, find all positive integers a1 such that the infinite sequence exists (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors). So we need to characterize the set of a1 for which the orbit never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This set might be exactly the set of numbers that are multiples of 6 and also have no prime factor less than something? But 30 is multiple of 6 but fails. So it's not just multiples of 6.

Let's try to understand the recurrence better. Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor is N/q where q is the second smallest divisor >1. The third largest is N/r where r is the third smallest divisor >1. So f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r. Note that p, q, r are divisors of N, and p<q<r. Also, note that N/p, N/q, N/r are proper divisors (since p,q,r>1). So f(N) is the sum of three proper divisors, but not necessarily the three largest? Actually, it is exactly the three largest proper divisors because d_{m-1}=N/p, d_{m-2}=N/q, d_{m-3}=N/r. So f(N) is the sum of the three largest proper divisors.

Now, we want to find all N such that the orbit stays in numbers with at least three proper divisors. This is a kind of "good" set. Possibly the only numbers that lead to infinite sequences are those that are "3-perfect" numbers (fixed points) and also numbers that are "preimages" of these fixed points under f. So far, no 2-cycle. Could there be a cycle of length >2? Possibly, but we haven't seen any. It seems that most numbers eventually hit a prime or a number with <3 proper divisors. The only numbers that seem to avoid that are the fixed points and possibly some numbers that map to fixed points. But also, maybe 72 leads to 78, which is fixed. So the sequence starting from 72 is: 72,78,78,78,... which is infinite. So a1=72 works.

But wait, we also found that 84 leads to 91->21->15->9 stop, so not good. 96 leads to 104->91->... stop. So 72 is special.

Now, we need to determine all possible a1. So far, we have: a1=6,18,42,54,66,78,102,126,138,162,174,198,222,234,258,282, etc. All numbers of the form 6k where k is odd and not multiple of 5. Also, a1=72 works. Are there any other numbers that are not fixed points but map to a fixed point? Let's test N=84? No. N=90? No. N=96? No. N=108? No. N=120? No. N=132? No. N=144? No. N=150? So the only numbers that are not fixed points but good are those that map to a fixed point in one step. So we need to find all N such that f(N) is a fixed point. We have found 72 gives 78. Are there others? Let's try to solve f(N) = 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5. Let's denote f(N)=M, where M is a fixed point. So M = 6k, k odd, 5∤k. We need to find all N such that f(N)=M. Thus, it seems that the only non-fixed point that maps to a fixed point is 72 for 78. Could there be a preimage of 72? We already tried to solve f(N)=72 and found none. So 72 is the only non-fixed point in the basin of F.

Now, we need to verify that all numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5 indeed lead to infinite sequences. For such N, we have N divisible by 6, but not by 4 or 5. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6. So f(N)=N/2+N/3+1 = 3k+2k+1 = 5k+1. But we need to check that f(N) is also a fixed point? Actually, for N to be a fixed point, we need 5k+1 = 6k => k=1. So only N=6 is fixed. For other N, f(N) = 5k+1. Is 5k+1 a multiple of 6? Not necessarily. For example, N=18 (k=3): f=5*3+1=16, which is not a fixed point. Now, we need to present a rigorous proof. Let's outline the proof.

First, define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N, provided N has at least three proper divisors. We need to find all N such that the sequence a_n defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is infinite. This is equivalent to: for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors.

We analyze the structure of f.

Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Let the divisors of N be 1 = d_1 < d_2 < ... < d_m = N. Then the three largest proper divisors are d_{m-3}, d_{m-2}, d_{m-1}. Since d_i * d_{m+1-i} = N, we have d_{m-1} = N/d_2, d_{m-2} = N/d_3, d_{m-3} = N/d_4. So f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4. Note that d_2 is the smallest divisor >1, so it is the smallest prime factor of N. Also, d_3 and d_4 are the next smallest divisors.

Now, we need to understand the iteration.

Observation: If N is divisible by 2, then d_2 = 2. If N is also divisible by 3, then d_3 = 3 if 3 divides N and there is no divisor between 2 and 3? Actually, 3 is the next prime, so if 3|N, then d_3 = 3. If N is divisible by 4, then d_3 could be 4 if 4|N and 3 does not divide N. So we need to consider cases.

We want to characterize numbers N for which f(N) is defined and the orbit stays in the domain.

We can try to show that the only numbers that lead to infinite sequences are those for which N is a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5, and also 72. But we need to prove that.

Let's try to prove that if N has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) < N unless N is a fixed point? Actually, we have seen that for N=6, f=6; N=18, f=18; N=42, f=42; N=54, f=54; etc. So fixed points exist. Also, for N=72, f=78 > 72. So f(N) can be greater than N. So we need to show that the only numbers that can be in an infinite sequence are those that eventually become fixed points. And we need to show that the only numbers that eventually become fixed points are those in F ∪ {72}. But we also need to show that all numbers in F are indeed fixed points (i.e., f(N)=N). So we need to prove that if N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5, then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6. That is true because the only divisors less than 6 are 2 and 3, and 6 is a divisor. So d_2=2, d_3=3, d_4=6. Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N. So N is fixed.

Now, we need to show that if N is not of that form, then either f(N) is not defined (i.e., N has fewer than three proper divisors) or the orbit eventually hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors, unless N=72. Let's try to analyze the transformation more systematically.

We have f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4, where d_2, d_3, d_4 are the three smallest divisors >1. Since d_2 is the smallest prime factor, we have d_2 = p, the smallest prime factor. Instead, we can use a more direct approach: Show that if N is not a fixed point, then the sequence eventually becomes strictly decreasing until it reaches a prime, unless N=72. And then show that the only numbers that can lead to a fixed point are those in F and 72. This is a typical "iterated function" problem.

Let's try to prove that for any N that is not a fixed point, either f(N) < N or f(N) is a fixed point. But we have seen that for N=30 (multiple of 6, divisible by 5), f(30)=31, which is prime, so f(N) is not defined further. So the sequence terminates. For N=36 (multiple of 6, divisible by 4), f(36)=39, which is not a fixed point, and f(39)=17, which is prime. So it decreases. So the only way to get an infinite sequence is if the sequence eventually becomes constant at a fixed point. So we need to find all N such that iterating f eventually hits a fixed point. This is like the set of numbers that are in the "preimage tree" of F.

We can try to prove that if N is not in F and N≠72, then f(N) is either a prime or a number that is not a multiple of 6, and then the process leads to a prime. But we need to handle the possibility that f(N) might be a multiple of 6 but not in F. For example, N=30 gives f=31 prime. N=36 gives f=39, not multiple of 6. N=48 gives f=52, not multiple of 6. N=60 gives f=65, not multiple of 6. N=72 gives f=78, which is in F. N=84 gives f=91, not multiple of 6. N=90 gives f=93, not multiple of 6. N=96 gives f=104, not multiple of 6. N=108 gives f=117, not multiple of 6. N=120 gives f=130, not multiple of 6. So it seems that for N=6k with k even, f(N)=13k/2, which is not a multiple of 6 unless k is a multiple of 12? Actually, 13k/2 = 6m => k=12m/13, so k must be divisible by 12? For m=13, k=12, so N=72. So only N=72 gives f(N) as a multiple of 6. For N=6k with k divisible by 5 but not by 4, f(N)=31k/5, which is not a multiple of 6 unless k is a multiple of 30? Actually, 31k/5 = 6m => k=30m/31, so k must be divisible by 30? For m=31, k=30, so N=180. But 180 is divisible by 4, so it's in the first case. So no. So only N=72 gives f(N) as a multiple of 6 and not in F? Actually, 78 is in F. So only N=72 gives f(N) as a fixed point.

Thus, the only numbers that can map to a fixed point in one step are those in F themselves and 72. And numbers in F map to themselves. So the basin of attraction of F is exactly F ∪ {72}. Now, we need to show that for any N not in F ∪ {72}, the orbit eventually hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This can be done by induction on the number of steps? Let's craft the solution.

We'll use mathematical notation.

We'll start with definitions.

Let's write:

Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$, provided $N$ has at least three proper divisors. For each $n\ge 1$, $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$. We need to find all positive integers $a_1$ such that the sequence is infinite.

First, note that if $N$ has at least three proper divisors, then we can list all divisors $1=d_1<d_2<\dots<d_m=N$. The three largest proper divisors are $d_{m-3},d_{m-2},d_{m-1}$. Since $d_i\cdot d_{m+1-i}=N$, we have $d_{m-1}=N/d_2$, $d_{m-2}=N/d_3$, $d_{m-3}=N/d_4$ (with the understanding that $d_4=N$ if $m<4$). Thus,
$$f(N)=\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}+\frac{N}{d_4}.$$

Now, we analyze the possible values.

**Lemma 1.** If $N$ is not a multiple of $6$, then $f(N)<N$ and $f(N)$ is not a multiple of $6$.

*Proof.* Suppose $2\nmid N$. Then $N$ is odd, so all its divisors are odd. Hence $f(N)$ is odd, so it cannot be a multiple of $2$, thus not a multiple of $6$. Also, because $N$ has at least three proper divisors, it is composite and $d_2\ge 3$. If $d_2>3$, then $N/d_2\le N/4$ and $N/d_3\le N/5$, $N/d_4\le N/7$, so $f(N)\le N/4+N/5+N/7< N$ (since $1/4+1/5+1/7=0.25+0.2+0.1429=0.5929<1$). If $d_2=3$, then $N$ is divisible by $3$ and $N$ is odd, so $N=3k$ with $k$ odd. Since $N$ has at least three proper divisors, $k>1$ and $k$ is not prime? Actually, $N$ could be $3p$ with $p$ prime? For $N=15$, only two proper divisors, so not considered. So $N$ must have at least three proper divisors, so $N$ is not a prime power with exponent $2$? For $N=27$, $k=9$, $N=27$ has divisors $1,3,9,27$, so $d_2=3$, $d_3=9$, $d_4=27$. Then $f=9+3+1=13<27$. In general, if $d_2=3$, then $d_3\ge 5$ and $d_4\ge 7$ (if $N$ is not a power of $3$), or $d_3=9$ and $d_4=27$ if $N=3^3$? But we can bound $f(N)=N/3+N/d_3+N/d_4\le N/3+N/5+N/7= N(1/3+1/5+1/7)= N(35/105+21/105+15/105)= N(71/105)<N$. So $f(N)<N$. Thus, in all cases, $f(N)<N$. ∎

**Lemma 2.** If $N$ is a multiple of $6$, then either $f(N)=N$ or $f(N)>N$.

*Proof.* Write $N=6k$. Then $d_2=2$, $d_3=3$. The next smallest divisor $d_4$ is either $4$ (if $4\mid N$), $5$ (if $5\mid N$ but $4\nmid N$), or $6$ (if $4\nmid N$ and $5\nmid N$). In the first two cases, $f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12$ or $N/2+N/3+N/5=31N/30$, both $>N$. In the third case, $f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N$. ∎

But we need integer: since 5|t, say t=5s, then f=31*78*5s/5=31*78s=2418s. So f(78t)=2418s. Now, 2418s is a multiple of 6? 2418=6*403, so yes. So we need to analyze further.

Thus, the process can continue. So the set of numbers that eventually become fixed points is those that, under repeated application of the transformation, eventually reach a number that is a fixed point (i.e., of the form 6k with k odd and 5∤k). This is like the set of numbers that are in the "preimage tree" of the fixed point set. Since the transformation is deterministic, we can try to characterize the set of numbers that are "good" by considering the prime factorization and the transformation rules.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, there might be a simpler characterization: all possible a1 are numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and N is not divisible by 4 or 5, plus also numbers that are multiples of 72? But we saw 144 is a multiple of 72 but does not work. So it's not that.

Let's compute the orbit for some numbers to see the pattern.

This condition is reminiscent of numbers that are "3-perfect" in the sense of sum of three largest divisors equals the number. But we need to characterize all such numbers.

We need to find all a1 such that the sequence is infinite. Since the recurrence is deterministic, the sequence is infinite if and only if every term has at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all N such that f(N) also has at least three proper divisors, and so on. But it's possible that some N lead to a cycle that never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. For example, could there be a cycle of length >1? Let's explore the transformation more generally.

Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Let the proper divisors be d1 < d2 < ... < d_k, with k>=3. Then the three largest are d_{k-2}, d_{k-1}, d_k. Their sum is f(N). Note that d_k = N/p where p is the smallest prime factor of N? Actually, the largest proper divisor is N divided by the smallest prime factor. But careful: If N is a prime power? For N=8, smallest prime factor=2, N/2=4, which is the largest proper divisor. For N=9, smallest prime factor=3, N/3=3, but 3 is not a proper divisor? Actually, 9's proper divisors: 1,3. Actually, the divisors come in pairs: if d is a divisor, then N/d is also a divisor. The largest proper divisor is N/p. The next largest proper divisor is either N/p^2 if p^2 divides N and p^2 is the next smallest divisor? Or N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor? For N=12, p=2, N/p=6; next smallest divisor >1 is 3, N/3=4; so second largest=4. For N=18, p=2, N/2=9; next smallest divisor >1 is 3, N/3=6; but 6 is not the second largest? That next smallest divisor could be p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime factor. So in general, if we list the divisors of N in increasing order: 1 = d1 < d2 < ... < d_k < N, then the three largest are N/d_{k-2}, N/d_{k-1}, N/d_k? Wait, if N has divisors including N, then the list of all divisors: 1 = d1 < d2 < ... < d_{m-1} < d_m = N. Then proper divisors are d1,...,d_{m-1}. So the three largest proper divisors are d_{m-3}, d_{m-2}, d_{m-1}. And note that d_{m-1} = N / d_2? Because d_2 * d_{m-1} = N. Similarly, d_{m-2} = N / d_3, and d_{m-3} = N / d_4. So the three largest proper divisors are N/d_2, N/d_3, N/d_4. So f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4, where d_2, d_3, d_4 are the three smallest proper divisors greater than 1? Actually, d_2 is the smallest divisor >1, which is the smallest prime factor p. d_3 is the next smallest divisor >1, which could be p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime factor q if p^2 does not divide N. d_4 is the next smallest divisor >1. So we have f(N) = N/p + N/d_3 + N/d_4.

Thus, the transformation depends on the three smallest divisors >1.

Now, we need to find all N such that the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. Since the recurrence is deterministic, we can try to characterize the set of numbers that are "good" (i.e., have at least three proper divisors and their f(N) also has at least three proper divisors, etc.). Possibly the only such numbers are those that are "3-perfect" (fixed points) and maybe some numbers that eventually lead to a fixed point. But we need to determine all possible a1 that can start such an infinite sequence. So we need to find all N such that the orbit under f is infinite. This includes numbers that eventually become fixed points, and also numbers that are part of a cycle. But we haven't found any cycles yet. Let's search for cycles.

We can try to compute f for numbers up to some range and see if any cycle appears. Since we have a computer-like reasoning, we can try to reason theoretically.

Define S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. We want to find all N such that f(N) is defined (i.e., N has at least 3 proper divisors) and then f(N) also has at least 3 proper divisors, etc. So we need to understand the behavior of f.

Observation: For N > 6, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is at most something. Let's analyze.

Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The next largest proper divisor is at most N/2? Let's analyze the transformation more generally. For N with at least 3 proper divisors, let the three smallest divisors >1 be p, q, r (with p<q<r). Then f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r. Note that p is the smallest prime factor of N. Also, q is the next smallest divisor >1. It could be p^2 if p^2|N, or the next prime factor. r is the next smallest divisor after q. So f(N) depends on the divisor structure.

We want to find all N such that iterating f eventually stays in numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. Since the fixed points are all numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5, we need to see if any number that is not of that form can eventually reach such a fixed point. So we need to find all N such that the orbit eventually hits a fixed point. This is reminiscent of numbers that are "3-smooth" or something? So 72 is a special preimage. Are there other preimages of fixed points? For example, consider N such that f(N) = 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5. We need to find all N for which f(N) is a fixed point. Then we can iterate: if a1 leads to a fixed point after some steps, then it's good. So the set of good a1 is the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. Since the fixed points are all numbers of that form, and the recurrence is deterministic, we need to find all numbers in the preimage tree of these fixed points. However, the problem asks: "Determine all possible values of a1." That is, find all positive integers a1 such that the infinite sequence exists (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors). So we need to characterize the set of a1 for which the orbit never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This set might be exactly the set of numbers that are multiples of 6 and also have no prime factor less than something? But 30 is multiple of 6 but fails. So it's not just multiples of 6.

Let's try to understand the recurrence better. Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor is N/q where q is the second smallest divisor >1. The third largest is N/r where r is the third smallest divisor >1. So f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r. Note that p, q, r are divisors of N, and p<q<r. Also, note that N/p, N/q, N/r are proper divisors (since p,q,r>1). So f(N) is the sum of three proper divisors, but not necessarily the three largest? Actually, it is exactly the three largest proper divisors because d_{m-1}=N/p, d_{m-2}=N/q, d_{m-3}=N/r. So f(N) is the sum of the three largest proper divisors.

Now, we want to find all N such that the orbit stays in numbers with at least three proper divisors. This is a kind of "good" set. Possibly the only numbers that lead to infinite sequences are those that are "3-perfect" numbers (fixed points) and also numbers that are "preimages" of these fixed points under f. Divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,9,12,18,36. The three smallest >1: 2,3,4. So p=2,q=3,r=4. Then f(36)=36/2+36/3+36/4 = 18+12+9=39. So 39=3*13, proper: 1,3,13; f=1+3+13=17. 17 prime -> stop. So 36 leads to dead end. 72=2^3*3^2. Divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,36,72. Three smallest >1: 2,3,4. So f(72)=72/2+72/3+72/4=36+24+18=78. 78 is fixed. So why does 72 work while 36 fails? The difference is that 36/2=18, 36/3=12, 36/4=9 sum=39; 72/2=36, 72/3=24, 72/4=18 sum=78. So the sum is 39 vs 78. 39 is not a fixed point, but 78 is. So the condition for N to be good is that f(N) is a fixed point. So we need to find all N such that f(N) is a fixed point. Then also numbers that map to those N, etc.

So we need to characterize the set of numbers N for which f(N) is a fixed point. Let's denote F = { N | f(N) = N and N has at least 3 proper divisors } = {6k | k odd, 5∤k}. So we need to find all N such that f(N) ∈ F. Then the set of good a1 is the set of numbers that eventually map to F under iteration of f. This is like the preimage of F under f. Since f is not injective, there could be many.

Let's try to find all N such that f(N) = 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5. Let's denote N's three smallest divisors >1 as p,q,r. Then f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r. We need this to be a multiple of 6 and also satisfy the condition that it is not divisible by 4 or 5? Actually, we need f(N) to be in F, so f(N) must be divisible by 6, and not divisible by 4 or 5. So we need N/p+N/q+N/r ≡ 0 mod 6, and also not divisible by 4 or 5. But maybe we can find a characterization.

Let's try to find N such that f(N) = 6. That would require N/p+N/q+N/r = 6. Since p,q,r >=2, the sum is at least 2+2+2=6, so equality only if p=q=r=2? But then N/2+N/2+N/2 = 3N/2 =6 => N=4. But N=4 has only proper divisors 1,2 (2 proper divisors), so not allowed. So no.

f(N)=18: N/p+N/q+N/r=18. We already saw N=18 works (p=2,q=3,r=6). Are there other N? Let's try to solve. Since 18 is even, N is even. Let p=2. Then N/2+N/q+N/r=18. Multiply 2: N+2N/q+2N/r=36. Then N/2+N/3+N/r=18 => N/2+N/3=18-N/r => multiply 6: 3N+2N=108-6N/r => 5N=108-6N/r => N=(108-6N/r)/5. Since N integer, 108-6N/r must be divisible by 5. Also, N/r is a proper divisor, so N/r >=? Also, if r is the third smallest divisor, then N must not have any divisor between 3 and r. So if r=6, then N must not be divisible by 4 or 5. So N=6k with k odd and not multiple of 5. Then N/2=3k, N/3=2k, N/6=k. So f(N)=3k+2k+k=6k=N. So f(N)=N, not 18. So for N to have triple (2,3,r) with r not 6, we need that N has a divisor r such that r>3 and there is no divisor between 3 and r. The possible r could be 4 if N is divisible by 4, but then 4 is a divisor, so triple would be (2,3,4) if 4 divides N? Actually, if N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor, and since 3 is also a divisor, the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4 (if N is not divisible by 5? But 4 is less than 5, so yes). If q=4, then N/2+N/4+N/r=18. Multiply 4: 2N+N+N*4/r=72 => 3N+N*4/r=72 => N(3+4/r)=72. Since r>q=4, and r divides N. Also, N must have p=2, q=4, so N divisible by 4. Now, we need to present a rigorous proof. Let's outline the proof.

First, define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N, provided N has at least three proper divisors. We need to find all N such that the sequence a_n defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is infinite. This is equivalent to: for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors.

We analyze the structure of f.

Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Let the divisors of N be 1 = d_1 < d_2 < ... < d_m = N. Then the three largest proper divisors are d_{m-3}, d_{m-2}, d_{m-1}. Since d_i * d_{m+1-i} = N, we have d_{m-1} = N/d_2, d_{m-2} = N/d_3, d_{m-3} = N/d_4. So f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4. Note that d_2 is the smallest divisor >1, so it is the smallest prime factor of N. Also, d_3 and d_4 are the next smallest divisors.

Now, we need to understand the iteration.

Observation: If N is divisible by 2, then d_2 = 2. If N is also divisible by 3, then d_3 = 3 if 3 divides N and there is no divisor between 2 and 3? Actually, 3 is the next prime, so if 3|N, then d_3 = 3. If N is divisible by 4, then d_3 could be 4 if 4|N and 3 does not divide N. So we need to consider cases.

We want to characterize numbers N for which f(N) is defined and the orbit stays in the domain.

We can try to show that the only numbers that lead to infinite sequences are those for which N is a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5, and also 72. But we need to prove that.

Let's try to prove that if N has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) < N unless N is a fixed point? Actually, we have seen that for N=6, f=6; N=18, f=18; N=42, f=42; N=54, f=54; etc. So fixed points exist. Also, for N=72, f=78 > 72. So f(N) can be greater than N. So we need to show that the only numbers that can be in an infinite sequence are those that eventually become fixed points. And we need to show that the only numbers that eventually become fixed points are those in F ∪ {72}. But we also need to show that all numbers in F are indeed fixed points (i.e., f(N)=N). We'll start with definitions.

Let's write:

Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$, provided $N$ has at least three proper divisors. For each $n\ge 1$, $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$. We need to find all positive integers $a_1$ such that the sequence is infinite.

First, note that if $N$ has at least three proper divisors, then we can list all divisors $1=d_1<d_2<\dots<d_m=N$. The three largest proper divisors are $d_{m-3},d_{m-2},d_{m-1}$. Since $d_i\cdot d_{m+1-i}=N$, we have $d_{m-1}=N/d_2$, $d_{m-2}=N/d_3$, $d_{m-3}=N/d_4$ (with the understanding that $d_4=N$ if $m<4$). Thus,
$$f(N)=\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}+\frac{N}{d_4}.$$

Now, we analyze the possible values.

**Lemma 1.** If $N$ is not a multiple of $6$, then $f(N)<N$ and $f(N)$ is not a multiple of $6$.

*Proof.* Suppose $2\nmid N$. Then $N$ is odd, so all its divisors are odd. Hence $f(N)$ is odd, so it cannot be a multiple of $2$, thus not a multiple of $6$. Also, because $N$ has at least three proper divisors, it is composite and $d_2\ge 3$. If $d_2>3$, then $N/d_2\le N/4$ and $N/d_3\le N/5$, $N/d_4\le N/7$, so $f(N)\le N/4+N/5+N/7< N$ (since $1/4+1/5+1/7=0.25+0.2+0.1429=0.5929<1$). If $d_2=3$, then $N$ is divisible by $3$ and $N$ is odd, so $N=3k$ with $k$ odd. Since $N$ has at least three proper divisors, $k>1$ and $k$ is not prime? Actually, $N$ could be $3p$ with $p$ prime? For $N=15$, only two proper divisors, so not considered. So $N$ must have at least three proper divisors, so $N$ is not a prime power with exponent $2$? For $N=27$, $k=9$, $N=27$ has divisors $1,3,9,27$, so $d_2=3$, $d_3=9$, $d_4=27$. Then $f=9+3+1=13<27$. In general, if $d_2=3$, then $d_3\ge 5$ and $d_4\ge 7$ (if $N$ is not a power of $3$), or $d_3=9$ and $d_4=27$ if $N=3^3$? But we can bound $f(N)=N/3+N/d_3+N/d_4\le N/3+N/5+N/7= N(1/3+1/5+1/7)= N(35/105+21/105+15/105)= N(71/105)<N$. So $f(N)<N$. Thus, in all cases, $f(N)<N$. ∎

**Lemma 2.** If $N$ is a multiple of $6$, then either $f(N)=N$ or $f(N)>N$.

*Proof.* Write $N=6k$. Then $d_2=2$, $d_3=3$. The next smallest divisor $d_4$ is either $4$ (if $4\mid N$), $5$ (if $5\mid N$ but $4\nmid N$), or $6$ (if $4\nmid N$ and $5\nmid N$). In the first two cases, $f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12$ or $N/2+N/3+N/5=31N/30$, both $>N$. In the third case, $f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N$. ∎

Actually, 36 is divisible by 12? 12*3=36, yes. So k=36 is a multiple of 12. So a1=216 is of the form 72t with t=3. So it is in the subcase where k is a multiple of 12. But we just found that for t=3, f(216)=234, which is a fixed point. So the orbit is infinite. So our earlier claim that for t>1 the orbit is finite is false. So we need to re-evaluate.

Thus, the analysis for N=6k with k even (i.e., N divisible by 4) is more subtle. We have f(N)=13k/2. If k is a multiple of 12, then N=72t, f=78t. Now, 78t is a multiple of 6. Its behavior depends on t. If t is odd and not divisible by 5, then 78t is a fixed point (since 78t=6*13t, 13t odd and 5∤13t). So the sequence becomes constant after one step. If t is odd and divisible by 5, then 78t is divisible by 5? Actually, 78t is divisible by 5 if t is multiple of 5. Then 78t is a multiple of 6 and divisible by 5, but not by 4 (since 78 not divisible by 4). So for such t, f(78t)=31*(78t)/30 = 31*78t/30 = 31*13t/5 = 403t/5? Wait, compute: 78t/30 = 13t/5, so f=31*13t/5 = 403t/5. For this to be integer, t must be multiple of 5. So if t=5, then f=403, which is prime? 403=13*31, so not prime. But we need to see if the orbit becomes finite. For t=5, a1=72*5=360, f(360)=? 360=6*60, 60 even, so f=13*360/12=13*30=390. 390=6*65, 65 odd, 5|65, so 390 is divisible by 5? 390/5=78, so 390 is divisible by 5. Also, 390 is even, so divisible by 2. Is 390 divisible by 4? 390/4=97.5, no. So 390 is a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5? Actually, 390 is divisible by 5, so it is divisible by 5. So it falls into the case of being a multiple of 6 and divisible by 5 but not by 4. Then f(390)=31*390/30=31*13=403. 403=13*31, not multiple of 6. Then f(403)=? 403=13*31, odd, not multiple of 3? 403 mod 3 = 1, so not multiple of 3. So f(403)=? Divisors: 1,13,31,403. f=31+13+1=45. 45=3^2*5, not multiple of 6? 45 mod 2=1, so not. f(45)=? 45's divisors: 1,3,5,9,15,45. Largest three: 15,9,5 sum=29. 29 prime. So sequence terminates. So a1=360 leads to finite orbit. So t=5 gives finite.

If t is even, then 78t is divisible by 4? 78t is even, and if t even, then 78t is divisible by 4? 78t = 2*39t, so divisible by 4 if 39t even, i.e., t even. So if t even, then 78t is divisible by 4. Then f(78t)=13*(78t)/12 = 169t/2. Since t even, say t=2s, then f=169s. Now, 169s is not a multiple of 6 unless s is a multiple of 6? Actually, 169s mod 2 = s mod 2, so it's even only if s even. So if s is even, then 169s is divisible by 2, but also need divisible by 3. 169≡1 mod 3, so 169s mod 3 = s mod 3. So for 169s to be a multiple of 6, we need s even and multiple of 3. So if s is not a multiple of 6, then 169s is not a multiple of 6. Then its orbit is finite by Lemma 1. If s is a multiple of 6, say s=6u, then t=12u, and a1=72t=864u. Then f(78t)=169s=169*6u=1014u. Now, 1014u is a multiple of 6? 1014=6*169, so 1014u is divisible by 6. And is it divisible by 4 or 5? 1014 is even, but not divisible by 4 (1014/4=253.5), and not divisible by 5. So 1014u is a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5. Then f(1014u)=1014u, a fixed point. So the sequence from a1=864u would be: a1=864u, f(a1)=78t=78*12u=936u? Wait, careful: a1=72t=72*12u=864u. Then f(a1)=13a1/12=13*864u/12=13*72u=936u. Then f(936u)=? 936u is divisible by 4? 936/4=234, so yes, 936u is divisible by 4. So we need to compute further. This seems to be a pattern: numbers of the form 72 * (multiple of 12) might lead to fixed points after a few steps. So we need to characterize all numbers that eventually map to a fixed point.

Thus, the set of good a1 is not just F ∪ {72}. It includes numbers like 216, 864, etc. So we need to find all a1 such that the orbit eventually becomes constant at a fixed point. This is a more complex classification.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, likely the answer is that a1 must be of the form 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5, plus also numbers that are multiples of 72? But 216 is a multiple of 72? 216/72=3, so yes, 216 is a multiple of 72. So maybe all multiples of 72 work? Let's test 72*2=144. We computed f(144)=156, then 156->? 156=6*26, 26 even, so f(156)=13*156/12=13*13=169, then 169->13+1+? Actually, 169's proper divisors: 1,13 (2 proper divisors), so f(169) undefined. So sequence terminates. So 144 does not work. So not all multiples of 72.

Thus, the pattern is more intricate.

Let's systematically analyze the transformation for numbers that are multiples of 6.

We have f(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r, where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1. For N=6k, we have p=2, q=3, and r = d_4 is the next smallest divisor after 3. So f(6k) = 3k + 2k + N/d_4 = 5k + N/d_4, but careful: N/d_4 = 6k/d_4. So f(6k) = 3k + 2k + 6k/d_4 = 5k + 6k/d_4. Since d_4 is a divisor of N, and N=6k, d_4 divides 6k. Also, d_4 > 3. The possible d_4 are: 4 if 4|N, 5 if 5|N but 4∤N, 6 if 4∤N and 5∤N. So:

- If 4|N, then d_4=4, and f(6k)=5k+6k/4=5k+3k/2= (10k+3k)/2=13k/2.
- If 5|N but 4∤N, then d_4=5, and f(6k)=5k+6k/5= (25k+6k)/5=31k/5.
- If 4∤N and 5∤N, then d_4=6, and f(6k)=5k+6k/6=5k+k=6k=N.

Thus, for N=6k, we have:

- If k is even, then 4|N, so f(N)=13k/2.
- If k is odd but 5|k, then 5|N, so f(N)=31k/5.
- If k is odd and 5∤k, then f(N)=N.

Now, we need to iterate. The sequence is infinite if and only if it never reaches a number with <3 proper divisors. Since the transformation can increase or decrease, we need to find all N such that the orbit eventually becomes constant at a fixed point.

We can try to characterize the set of numbers that are "good" (i.e., eventually reach a fixed point). Let's denote the set G = { N | the orbit is infinite }. We want to find G.

We have already shown that if N is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence strictly decreases and is finite. So G ⊆ { multiples of 6 }.

Now, let N = 6k. If k is odd and 5∤k, then N is a fixed point, so N ∈ G. So all such N are in G.

If k is even, then N is divisible by 4, so f(N)=13k/2. Now, we need to analyze the orbit from f(N). Let M = 13k/2. Since k even, write k=2m, then M=13m. Now, M is a multiple of 13, and it is not necessarily a multiple of 6. We need to see when the orbit from M is infinite. If M is not a multiple of 6, then by Lemma 1, its orbit is finite, so the orbit from N is finite. If M is a multiple of 6, then we can apply the same analysis. So we need to find all k even such that M=13k/2 is a multiple of 6. That is, 13k/2 ≡ 0 mod 6 => 13k/2 divisible by 2 and 3. Since 13 is odd, we need k/2 to be divisible by 2 and 3? Actually, 13k/2 divisible by 2 means k/2 is even, i.e., k divisible by 4. And divisible by 3 means k/2 divisible by 3, i.e., k divisible by 6. So k must be divisible by lcm(4,6)=12. So k=12t. Then M=13*12t/2=78t. So M=78t. Now, M is a multiple of 6. So we need to analyze the orbit from M. So the condition for N=6k with k even to possibly lead to infinite orbit is that k is a multiple of 12. Then N=72t, and f(N)=78t. So we need to determine when the orbit from 78t is infinite.

Now, 78t is a multiple of 6. Write 78t = 6 * (13t). So k' = 13t. Now, we apply the same classification to 78t. For 78t, we need to check its divisibility by 4 and 5. Since 78 is not divisible by 4, 78t is divisible by 4 iff t is even. And 78t is divisible by 5 iff t is a multiple of 5 (since 78 not divisible by 5). So:

- If t is even, then 78t is divisible by 4. Then f(78t)=13*(78t)/12 = 169t/2. So we need to analyze f(78t).
- If t is odd, then 78t is not divisible by 4. Then we check if 5|t. If 5∤t, then 78t is not divisible by 4 or 5, so f(78t)=78t, a fixed point. So then the orbit from 78t is constant, so infinite. If 5|t, then 78t is divisible by 5 but not by 4, so f(78t)=31*(78t)/5 = 31*78t/5 = 31*13t/5? Actually, 78t/5 = 78t/5, so f=31*(78t/5)= (31*78t)/5 = 2418t/5? But we need integer: since 5|t, say t=5s, then f=31*78*5s/5=31*78s=2418s. So f(78t)=2418s. Now, 2418s is a multiple of 6? 2418=6*403, so yes. So we need to analyze further.

Thus, the process can continue. So the set of numbers that eventually become fixed points is those that, under repeated application of the transformation, eventually reach a number that is a fixed point (i.e., of the form 6k with k odd and 5∤k). This is like the set of numbers that are in the "preimage tree" of the fixed point set. Since the transformation is deterministic, we can try to characterize the set of numbers that are "good" by considering the prime factorization and the transformation rules.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, there might be a simpler characterization: all possible a1 are numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and N is not divisible by 4 or 5, plus also numbers that are multiples of 72? But we saw 144 is a multiple of 72 but does not work. So it's not that.

We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N (if N has at least three proper divisors).
- Show that if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N and f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Hence the sequence strictly decreases and eventually hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors, so the sequence is finite.
- Thus a_1 must be a multiple of 6. Write a_1 = 6k.
- If k is odd and 5 ∤ k, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6, so f(a_1)=a_1, giving a constant infinite sequence.
- If k is even, then a_1 is divisible by 4, and f(a_1)=13k/2. For the orbit to be infinite, we need f(a_1) to be a fixed point. This happens exactly when k is a multiple of 12, i.e., a_1 = 72t. For t=1, f(72)=78 which is fixed, so a_1=72 works. For t>1, we need to check further; we have found that some t>1 work (e.g., t=3 gives 216, f(216)=234 which is fixed) while others do not (e.g., t=2 gives 144, f(144)=156, and then f(156)=169, etc., leading to a dead end). The pattern is more complex; the set of good a_1 is exactly the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point under f. A full classification requires solving the iteration of f on multiples of 6. However, the problem asks for all possible a_1, so we need to find the set of numbers that lead to infinite sequence.
